# 太上灵宝元阳妙经

**The Supreme Numinous Treasure Primordial Yang Wondrous Scripture**

**Scripture Title:** *The Supreme Numinous Treasure Primordial Yang Wondrous Scripture* (abbreviated as *The Primordial Yang Scripture*).
**Author:** Unknown, approximately composed during the Northern and Southern Dynasties.
**Original Volume Count:** Unknown. Ten volumes survive (with the tenth partially reconstructed).
**Source Text:** *Daoist Canon of the Zhengtong Era*, *Dongxuan Section*, *Benwen Category*.

---

### **Table of Contents**
**Volume 1:** *The Sagely Conduct Chapter, Part One*
**Volume 2:** *The Sagely Conduct Chapter, Part Two*
**Volume 3:** *The Compassionate Conduct Chapter*
**Volume 4:** *The Inquiry into Conduct Chapter*
**Volume 5:** *The Contemplative Conduct Chapter, Part One*
**Volume 6:** *The Contemplative Conduct Chapter, Part Two*
**Volume 7:** *The Contemplative Conduct Chapter, Part Three*
**Volume 8:** *The Virtuous Conduct Chapter*
**Volume 9:** *The Noble and Exalted Virtuous Conduct Chapter*
**Volume 10:** *The Ascension to the Great Luo Chapter*

---

### **The Supreme Numinous Treasure Primordial Yang Wondrous Scripture, Volume 1**
**The Sagely Conduct Chapter, Part One**

At that time, the Perfected One of Primordial Yang, Radiant Wondrous Sound, heard that the Heavenly Worthy was in the Jeweled Palace of the Mysterious Capital, the Supreme Purple Tenuity Palace, the Wondrous Joy Realm, and the Primordial Yang World, expounding the Mahāyāna scriptural teachings to the assembled host of celestial great sages and flying spirit-kings. For the retinues of divine immortals, he elucidated the profound meanings, the subtle and wondrous scriptures, the divine transformations, and the inconceivable manifestations.

Having heard this, Wondrous Sound was filled with joy. He then led his eighty-four thousand disciples, saying: "I have now heard of a sagely honored one, whose majestic virtue is resplendent beyond compare. You must prepare precious incense and rare treasures, banners and canopies, palanquins and carriages, musical instruments and performances—all the requisites for offering. Tomorrow at noon, we shall seek out this luminous master."

Upon hearing these words, the disciples were each astonished and doubtful, marveling at what they had never before encountered. They swiftly prepared their journey, adorning themselves with coral and amber, luminous pearls and jeweled banners, fragrant flowers and the rarest treasures of the world. Their attendants and followers rode upon clouds and dragons, soaring through the void.

Upon arriving at the abode of the Heavenly Worthy, Wondrous Sound and his retinue trembled with awe, their movements unsettled. From among the assembly, a perfected one named Worthy Wisdom proclaimed in a loud voice: "We, your disciples, now adjust our robes and crowns, purify our sight and hearing, and set up a table in the empty space with hundred-fold blended incense. We array banners, jeweled canopies, and musical instruments, surrounding the honored one on all sides, filling the void."

Worthy Wisdom, clad in proper attire, held an incense burner, lowered his brows, gathered his knees, softened his breath, and harmonized his voice. Bowing his head in reverence, he advanced to make obeisance, inquiring after the celestial countenance and the honored one’s well-being. He then proclaimed the Dharma teachings, ensuring that virtue remained unharmed and harmony undisturbed.

Worthy Wisdom, by virtue of past causes, had fortunately been favored by the exalted perfected ones. Through the turning of karmic conditions, he had encountered the Dao’s dispensation. His joy in compassion was boundless, yet lingering obstructions from past lives still left him with deep doubts. Long immersed in delusion, he had no means to awaken. Now, hearing that the Heavenly Worthy had extended great merciful grace, opening the path of compassion, he had become a great vessel, a great ladder, a great enlightened master, and the supreme lord of the Dao for all the people—men and women, ordinary beings, and sentient beings.

Worthy Wisdom dared to rely upon the radiant majesty and this wondrous power to venture a humble inquiry into the doubts that weighed upon his heart. He prostrated himself, entreating the great master to take pity on the foolish and ignorant and deign to bestow his enlightening answers.

The Heavenly Worthy remained silent for a long while. Then, his face suffused with five-colored light, the three luminaries shone brilliantly, his seventy-two marks and eighty excellences illuminated the ten directions and the worlds as numerous as the sands of the Ganges. He emitted a great Dharma sound, resounding through the lands.

"Excellent! Excellent! Your questions are profound indeed. In accordance with what you have asked, I shall now resolve them."

Worthy Wisdom addressed the Heavenly Worthy, saying: "Since you have deigned to enlighten and awaken sentient beings, I venture to ask—what practices and vows must one cultivate to attain the fruit of the Dao?"

The Heavenly Worthy replied: "If one seeks to cultivate the wondrous natural Dao, one must first eliminate the six corrupting deeds."

The Perfected Worthy Wisdom then asked: "By what causes and conditions are these called the six corrupting deeds?"

The Heavenly Worthy answered: "The first is miserly greed; the second, breaking precepts; the third, anger and hatred; the fourth, laziness and sloth; the fifth, distraction and disorder; the sixth, ignorance and delusion. Those who cultivate the natural Dao must eliminate these six corrupting deeds—only then may the true Dao be attained.

"Furthermore, there are five contemplations of the body. What are these five? The first is contemplating the body as impure; the second, contemplating the body as suffering and affliction; the third, contemplating the body as impermanent; the fourth, contemplating all dharmas as without self; the fifth, contemplating the body as empty and quiescent, possessing nothing whatsoever. Good son, Perfected Ones and Daoists must constantly cultivate these five contemplations.

"Moreover, there is one practice—the Heavenly Worthy’s *Supreme Wondrous Scripture of Primordial Yang*. What is the sacred practice cultivated by the Perfected One? When a Perfected One or Daoist first hears this great scripture from the Heavenly Worthy, their heart arises in reverence and faith. Having revered and believed in the Dharma, they should cultivate the following reflection: There exists the supreme Dao, the great and correct Dharma, the scriptures of Primordial Yang. I now reflect that, for the sake of seeking what is most essential, I must relinquish my beloved wife and children, parents and siblings, the six relations and kin, my dwelling and abode, gold and silver, jewels and treasures, servants and slaves. I must further think thus: Dwelling at home is constricting, like being bound in fetters—all roots of transgression arise from it. Leaving the household is serene and free, like empty space—all causes of goodness and benefit depend upon it. If I remain at home, I cannot recite the scriptures of Primordial Yang, nor cultivate wisdom. I must now renounce love and attachment and leave the household to study the Dao.

"I must further resolve: I shall now decisively sever attachment and cultivate the supreme, correct, and natural fruit. When a Perfected One or Daoist desires to leave the household, various heavenly demons and malevolent spirits will arise to disturb and vex them—they must then engage in great struggle with me.

"Good son, how does such a Perfected One contend with others in struggle? At that time, the Perfected One or Daoist proceeds to the Palace of Primordial Yang. There, beholding the Heavenly Worthy and his disciples—whose majestic bearing is complete, whose faculties are pure, whose hearts are gentle and yielding, serene and free from action—they approach and seek to leave the household, casting aside secular robes and donning the vestments of the Dharma. Having left the household, they uphold the precepts of the Dharma, their majestic bearing unblemished, their conduct composed and dignified. They commit no transgressions, not even minor faults, and dare not entertain them in their hearts. They guard the precepts of the Dharma as a child guards its life.

"It is like a person carrying a gourd to cross a vast sea. A sea deity then appears and begs for the gourd. Hearing this, the person thinks: *If I give it to you, I will surely drown.* They reply to the sea deity: *You may kill me, but the gourd cannot be yours.* The sea deity persists: *If you cannot give it all to me, then give me half.* The person still refuses. The sea deity says: *If you cannot give me half, then a third.* The person again refuses. The sea deity then says: *Give me a piece the size of your hand.* The person still refuses. The sea deity pleads: *I am starving and freezing, suffering greatly—grant me even a thread’s worth.* The person still refuses, saying: *What you ask is not much, but I am now crossing the sea, uncertain of its bounds or how far the shore lies. Why should I give it to you? If I perish in the sea, what regret will avail me?*

"Good son, the Perfected One or Daoist who guards the precepts of the Dharma is likewise thus—like one crossing the sea, cherishing the gourd. When the Perfected One guards the precepts of the Dharma, the sea deities of affliction and evil constantly say to them: *Believe in me—I will not deceive you. Merely break the five precepts and uphold the Dharma—by this cause and condition, you will be secure and attain the immortal ranks of Primordial Yang.* At that time, they should reply: *I would rather uphold these precepts and fall into the hell of dark sands than break the precepts, violate the Dharma, and be reborn in heaven.*

"The sea deity of affliction then says: *If you cannot break the five precepts, then eat human flesh—by this cause and condition, you will be secure and attain the immortal ranks of Primordial Yang.* The Perfected One does not heed their words. The sea deity says: *If you cannot eat human flesh, then commit theft and robbery—by this cause and condition, you will be secure and attain the immortal ranks of Primordial Yang.* The Perfected One still does not comply. The sea deity says: *If you cannot commit theft and robbery, then commit the five heinous crimes—by this cause and condition, you may be secure and enter the immortal ranks of Primordial Yang.* The Perfected One again refuses. The sea deity says: *If you cannot commit the five heinous crimes, then enter the chamber of lust—by this cause and condition, you will be secure and attain the immortal ranks of Primordial Yang.* The Perfected One still does not comply. The sea deity says: *If you will not enter the chamber of lust, then break the four alliance precepts—by this cause and condition, you may be secure and enter the immortal ranks of Primordial Yang.*

"At that time, the Perfected One reflects in their heart: *If I break the four alliance precepts and do not confess and repent, I will be unable to cross the shore of birth and death and attain the immortal ranks of Primordial Yang.* Thus, the Perfected One or Daoist guards even the most minor precepts with unyielding resolve, their heart like adamant."

The Perfected One and Daoist masters uphold the one hundred and eighty precepts and the supreme wisdom precepts, honoring and revering them equally without distinction. If the Perfected One can firmly uphold these, they will be complete in the true Dao, dedicating their precepts to the Primordial Correct and True Dao.

At that time, the wise and virtuous Perfected One further observes the Five Register Precepts: first, not to kill living beings; second, not to steal others’ property; third, not to engage in illicit relations with others’ wives; fourth, not to speak harsh or false words; fifth, not to consume alcohol or meat. If the Perfected One cultivates these Five Register Precepts, they will never commit evil and will enter the joy of the unconditioned and truly eternal.

Furthermore, good man, there are two kinds of precepts: first, the precept of purifying the mind; second, the precept of ceasing worldly entanglements. The precept of purifying the mind means conducting oneself without the slightest defilement, with a resolve that does not transgress wrongdoing. The precept of ceasing worldly entanglements means: not engaging in the trade of treasures to seek blessings and joy; not borrowing others’ power and influence to elevate oneself; not seeking worldly meat flavors to sate hunger and emptiness; not delighting in ornate and luxurious clothing for adornment; not delighting in lofty and spacious dwellings, beds, mats, screens, curtains, felt blankets, brocade, and other such things. If one can sever all such matters, good man, this is called the precept of ceasing worldly entanglements. The Perfected One and Daoist masters uphold these prohibitive precepts equally without distinction.

Good man, when the Perfected One and Daoist masters receive and uphold such dharma precepts, they make this vow: *I would rather cast this body into fierce fire and deep pits than break the dharma precepts established by the Primordial Heavenly Worthy and act impurely like the common multitude of beings.*

Furthermore, good man, the Perfected One and Daoist masters make this vow: *I would rather have molten iron poured over my body than dare break the dharma and accept the clothing of faithful and virtuous ones.*

Furthermore, good man, the Perfected One and Daoist masters make this vow: *I would rather have this mouth swallow molten iron than dare break the dharma and consume the food and drink of faithful and virtuous ones.*

Furthermore, good man, the Perfected One and Daoist masters make this vow: *I would rather have this body lie upon a bed of hot iron than dare break the dharma and accept the bedding and furnishings of faithful and virtuous ones.*

Furthermore, good man, the Perfected One and Daoist masters make this vow: *I would rather have this body suffer from grave illness than dare break the dharma and accept the medicine of faithful and virtuous ones.*

Furthermore, good man, the Perfected One and Daoist masters make this vow: *I would rather cast this body into a cauldron of boiling iron than dare break the dharma and accept the houses and dwellings of faithful and virtuous ones.*

Furthermore, good man, the Perfected One and Daoist masters make this vow: *I would rather have an iron rod shatter this body than break the dharma and accept the reverence and bows of faithful and virtuous ones.*

Furthermore, good man, the Perfected One and Daoist masters make this vow: *I would rather have iron hooks gouge out my eyes than harbor impure thoughts and indulge in others’ beauty.*

Furthermore, good man, the Perfected One and Daoist masters make this vow: *I would rather have iron awls pierce my ears than harbor defiled thoughts and listen to pleasant sounds.*

Furthermore, good man, the Perfected One and Daoist masters make this vow: *I would rather have a sharp blade cut off my nose than harbor defiled thoughts and covet various fragrances.*

Furthermore, good man, the Perfected One and Daoist masters make this vow: *I would rather have a sharp blade sever my tongue than harbor defiled thoughts and crave various flavors.*

Furthermore, good man, the Perfected One and Daoist masters make this vow: *I would rather have an axe hew my body than harbor defiled thoughts and covet various tactile sensations.*

Why is this? For this reason: they vow that all beings may uphold the dharma precepts and attain the pure dharma.

Good man, when the Perfected One and Daoist masters cultivate such pure dharma, they immediately abide in the Unmoving Ground. What is the Unmoving Ground? The Perfected One abiding in this Unmoving Ground is unshaken and does not fall, does not retreat and does not scatter.

Good man, for example, the fierce winds of the Jade Capital Mountain cannot shake it; the Perfected One and Daoist masters abiding in this ground are likewise unmoved by forms, sounds, and flavors, nor scattered by perverse views and the winds of lust.

Good man and woman, *unmoving* means not being disturbed by greed, hatred, or delusion. *Not falling* means not falling into the three evil destinies. *Not retreating* means not retreating from the dharma and returning to the household life. *Not scattering* means not being scattered and destroyed by the retrogressive *Primordial Yang* scriptures.

Furthermore, the Perfected One and Daoist masters are also unmoved by the demon kings of afflictions, unshaken by evil spirits, and even when sitting beneath the bodhi tree in the grove of *Kan* trees, though demon kings may be present, they cannot cause them to retreat from the supreme, correct, and true great Dao, nor are they disturbed by the demon of death. This is called the Perfected One cultivating true practice.

Good man, what is *true practice*? True practice is what the Heavenly Worthy and all immortals practice, hence it is called true practice. For what reason are the Heavenly Worthy and Perfected Ones called *sages*? Such beings possess the dharma of sages and constantly contemplate the quiescent nature of all dharmas; for this reason, they are called Perfected Ones. Possessing the dharma of sages, they are called Perfected Ones. Possessing concentration and wisdom, they are called Perfected Ones. Possessing the seven true treasures—faith, dharma, shame, dread, extensive learning, wisdom, and renunciation—they are called Perfected Ones. For these reasons, it is also called true practice.

Furthermore, good man, the true practice of the Perfected One and Daoist masters is to observe that this body is impure, a temporary aggregation of causes and conditions. How does it arise, sit, stand, walk, bend, stretch, look, blink, breathe, weep, laugh, or rejoice? There is no master within—who causes it to be thus? Having asked this question, all the immortals in the assembly suddenly vanished.

The Perfected One further reflected: "Perhaps consciousness is the self, and thus the sages do not act on my behalf." Yet upon observing this consciousness, he saw it arise and cease in succession, like flowing water—this too was not the self.

He then thought: "If consciousness does not exist, perhaps the exhalation and inhalation of breath could be the self." But again he considered: "Exhalation and inhalation are merely the nature of wind, and wind is but one of the Four Great Elements. Among the Four Great Elements, which is truly real? The nature of earth is not real; the natures of water, fire, and wind are likewise unreal."

He further reflected: "This body in its entirety possesses no true reality. Only the mind and wind, through the convergence of causes and conditions, manifest in various forms. All its activities are like illusions conjured by spells, or like the sound of a bell, resonating as the mind wills. Thus, this body is impure, a mingled aggregation of causes and conditions—where in it could desire arise? If reviled, where in it could anger take root?

This body of mine, with its hundred impure apertures and six foul sense faculties—where in it could endure the reviler’s words? Upon hearing insults, one should contemplate: by what sound is one reviled? No single sound can constitute an insult; if one alone cannot, then many are likewise powerless. For what reason, then, should anger arise?

If another’s touch provokes, one should likewise reflect: from what does such contact arise? One then realizes: it arises from the mind’s action, which in turn affects this body—thus it is called ‘touch.’ Why should I unjustly resent another? It is this body itself that incurs the fault, for it is through the five aggregates that I experience it.

Just as a target gives rise to arrows, so too does this body. With body comes contact; if I cannot endure it, the mind becomes scattered. When the mind is scattered, right mindfulness is lost. Losing right mindfulness, one cannot discern what is real and what is not. Unable to discern reality, one commits evil deeds. Through the causes of evil deeds, one falls into the three evil destinies and the ten hells of unwholesome realms.

Having thus observed, the Perfected One attained the Four Foundations of Mindfulness. Having attained them, he could abide in the realm of forbearance. The Perfected One and Daoist master, dwelling in this realm, could endure desire and anger, as well as cold and heat, hunger and thirst, all manner of painful contacts, various diseases and epidemics, harsh words and curses, whippings and beatings, and all afflictions of body and mind—this is what it means to abide in the realm of forbearance.

The Perfected One Yuan Yang, Xianzhi, addressed the Heavenly Worthy, saying: "Daoist masters who have not yet attained the unshakable realm, while purely upholding the precepts, may under certain conditions commit transgressions against the Dharma."

"Good man," replied the Perfected One, "those who have not yet attained the unshakable realm may, under certain conditions, transgress the Dharma."

The Perfected One said: "Indeed."

"Heavenly Worthy, what are these conditions?"

The Heavenly Worthy told the Perfected One Xianzhi: "If a Perfected One or Daoist master knows the conditions for transgressing the Dharma, he can thereby enable others to receive, uphold, and delight in the Yuan Yang scriptures. He can also enable them to receive, uphold, recite, and master the words and phrases, to copy the scriptures, and to expound them widely to others. With unwavering resolve, he guides them toward the supreme, correct, and natural Great Dao. For this reason, transgression of the Dharma is permissible."

At that time, the Perfected One should reflect thus: "I would rather fall into the dark sand hells and endure suffering than allow this person to regress from the supreme, correct, and natural Great Dao."

Then, the Perfected Ones Yuan Yang, Xianfu, Zilin, and others addressed the Heavenly Worthy, saying: "If a Perfected One gathers and protects such a person, ensuring their unwavering resolve in the natural mind, it would be impossible for this to constitute a transgression of the Dharma or lead to a fall into the dark sand hells."

At that time, the Heavenly Worthy praised the Perfected One Xianzhi, saying: "Excellent! Excellent! As you have said, I recall that in the past, in the Jambudvīpa realm, I was a great king named Shoujian. I cherished and revered the Yuan Yang scriptures with a pure and virtuous heart, free from defilements such as jealousy, stinginess, and greed. My speech was always gentle, and my mind constantly protected the poor and orphaned. I practiced generosity and diligence without rest.

At a time when no sages existed in the world, I delighted in the supreme Mahāyāna scriptures of the Yuan Yang. For nine years, I served Daoist masters as my teachers, providing for all their needs. After nine years, having completed my offerings, I said: 'Masters, you should now arouse the mind for the supreme, correct, and natural Dharma.'

The Daoist masters replied: 'Great King, the nature of the natural is non-existent; the teachings of the Yuan Yang are likewise so. Great King, how can you wish to make others equal to emptiness?'

Good man, at that time, my delight in the wondrous Yuan Yang scriptures and my reverent recitation of them brought forth immeasurable merit.

Furthermore, good man, those who cultivate the sagely practice should know that conditioned dharmas involve two aspects: cause and effect. Unconditioned dharmas also involve two aspects: cause and effect. The effect of conditioned dharmas is called suffering; the cause of conditioned dharmas is called accumulation. The effect of unconditioned dharmas is called cessation; the cause of unconditioned dharmas is called the Dharma.

Moreover, good man, the Perfected One and Daoist master experience eight kinds of suffering: birth, aging, sickness, death, love, aversion, separation, and parting. That which gives rise to these eight sufferings is called accumulation. Where these eight do not exist is called cessation.

Good man, birth has five manifestations: first emergence, reaching completion, growth, emergence from the womb, and birth by kind. What is aging?"

Old age has two kinds: first, moment-to-moment aging; second, lifelong aging. Furthermore, there are two other kinds: first, aging through growth; second, aging through decay and dissolution. These are called old age.

For what reason is it called sickness? It is like venomous snakes within the body, each finding no comfort in the other. For this reason, there are also two kinds of sickness: first, bodily sickness; second, sickness of the mind.

Bodily sickness has five types. What are the five? First, sickness caused by water; second, sickness caused by wind; third, sickness caused by heat; fourth, sickness caused by mixed malign influences; fifth, sickness from external factors as guest afflictions.

Among these guest afflictions, there are four further kinds: first, sickness from forced actions against one’s will; second, sickness from forgetfulness or accidental falls; third, sickness from weapons, tiles, or stones; fourth, sickness from possession by demons or malevolent spirits.

Among sicknesses of the mind, there are also four kinds: first, the mind leaping with perverse excitement; second, the mind generating terror; third, the mind filled with sorrow; fourth, the mind’s nature being foolish and deluded.

Moreover, good man, the sicknesses of the mind and nature of Perfected Ones and Daoist masters have three further kinds. For what reason are they called three? First, the body and mind commit offenses and receive karmic retribution; second, one fails to distance oneself from all evil adversities and offenses; third, the four seasons alternate and decline. For these reasons, they are called discriminating sicknesses. All sicknesses are named as such when the mind and intent become confused and disordered. This is called sickness.

What is death? Death is the abandonment of the body one has received. The abandonment of the received body has two distinctions: first, death when lifespan is exhausted; second, death from contingent causes.

Death when lifespan is exhausted has two further conditions: first, lifespan is exhausted but merit is not; second, merit is exhausted but lifespan is not.

Death from contingent causes has two further meanings. What are the two meanings? First, self-inflicted death beyond one’s allotted portion; second, death from being harmed by others.

There are also three kinds of death: first, death from dissipation; second, death from destroying the Dharma; third, death from ruining life.

What is death from dissipation? If one slanders the supreme Mahāyāna scriptures of the Primordial Yang, this is called death from dissipation.

What is death from destroying the Dharma? Slandering the laws and precepts established by all the sages of the past, future, and present is called death from destroying the Dharma.

What is death from ruining life? Abandoning the five aggregates is called death from ruining life. Such deaths are called great suffering.

What is the suffering of separation from the beloved? The beloved objects are destroyed and scattered. This suffering of separation has two distinctions. What are the two? First, the intermediate state in the human realm; second, the intermediate state in the heavenly realm. The intermediate states experienced by humans and gods have countless varieties when differentiated and enumerated. This is called the suffering of love.

What is the suffering of encountering the despised? The accumulations have three kinds: first, hell; second, hungry ghosts; third, animals. These three destinies, when differentiated in number, are countless. Such is called the suffering of encountering the despised.

What is the suffering of not obtaining what is sought? There are two kinds: first, having aspirations but finding them difficult to attain; second, expending much effort yet gaining no merit. Such matters are called the suffering of not obtaining what is sought.

What is the suffering of the five aggregates? The suffering of the five aggregates comprises birth, old age, sickness, death, love, aversion, separation, and parting—these are the roots of affliction.

Good man, you should know that among the eight sufferings, the body is the greatest affliction. If one can blunt sharp desires, restrain emotions, and single-mindedly cultivate and venerate the supreme Mahāyāna scriptures of the Primordial Yang, one may escape these eight afflictions and the eight kinds of suffering and sorrow.

At that time, the Primordial Celestial Venerable addressed the Worthy and Wise Perfected One, saying: "All ordinary beings, with inverted and deluded minds, cling to the appearance of birth and detest separation from old age and sickness. I am not like this. Observing the moment of birth, I already see the afflictions of sickness.

Perfected One, there was once a woman named Good Faith who entered the house of a elder in the world of Pulin. This woman was upright, her countenance beautiful, adorned in worldly brocades, patterned silks, and fine garments. The elder, upon seeing her, immediately asked: 'What is your name? From where have you come?'"

The woman replied, "I am the great wealth-master of merit in this present world."

The elder asked, "What benefit do you bring to the places you visit?"

The woman said, "Wherever I go, I generate all manner of sweet fruits and delicacies, gold, silver, copper, iron, brocades, patterned silks, cattle, horses, carriages, male and female servants, and young attendants."

Upon hearing this, the elder rejoiced in his heart: "By past karmic conditions, I have encountered a good spiritual friend, for great benefit!" He then paid reverent homage, burned incense, scattered flowers, and bowed with utmost sincerity, wishing for compassion.

At that moment, outside the gate stood another woman, her form hideous, her garments foul and filthy. Seeing her, the elder asked, "Where do you come from?"

The woman replied, "I am called Black Karma."

The elder said, "For what reason are you called Black Karma?"

The woman answered, "Wherever I travel, I cause the decline and depletion of that household’s wealth."

Hearing this, the elder immediately took up a sharp blade and chased her away, saying, "If you linger, I will take your life."

Black Karma replied, "Do not be foolish, devoid of good heart."

The elder asked, "Why do you call me foolish, devoid of good heart?"

Black Karma answered, "The woman in your house named Good Faith is my elder sister. We always travel together. If you chase me away, you must also drive out my sister."

The elder returned inside and asked Good Faith, "Is the woman outside your younger sister?"

Good Faith replied, "She is indeed my sister. We sisters travel everywhere together, never parting, wherever we tread. I always do good, while she always does evil; I perform good karma, while she performs evil karma. Since you love me, you must also show compassion to her."

The elder replied, "If this is so, I want neither of you." He then hastily drove them away, urging them not to delay.

The two women then departed and returned to their dwelling. At that time, the elder’s heart was filled with joy, his delight boundless.

The two women then went together to a poor household. Seeing them, the poor man’s heart leapt with joy, and he invited them, saying, "From now on, may the two of you always stay in my home."

The women replied, "We have already been driven away by others. Why do you now wish to keep us both here?"

The poor man answered, "You think of me, and I think of you. That is why I invite you both to stay in my home."

Worthy and Wise Perfected One, it is likewise with you: you do not wish to be born in heaven, for birth entails old age, sickness, and death. Thus, you abandon both, having no attachment.

Ordinary beings, unaware of old age, sickness, and death, constantly cling to and crave the dual karma of birth and death.

Furthermore, Perfected One and Daoist, suppose a youth of wondrous wisdom, pressed by hunger, sees fruits in filth and excrement and immediately takes them. A wise person, seeing this, rebukes him: "You, a youth of wondrous wisdom, of pure and noble nature—why do you take these fruits from filth?"

Hearing this, the youth of wondrous wisdom felt deep shame and replied, "I do not truly intend to eat them. I only wished to wash them clean and then discard them."

The wise person said, "You are greatly foolish and benighted. If you discard them, you should not have taken them in the first place."

Good son, Perfected One and Daoist, it is likewise with you: in this life, you neither take nor discard, just as the wise person rebuked the youth of wondrous wisdom.

Ordinary beings rejoice in birth and detest death, like that youth of wondrous wisdom who took the fruit only to discard it.

Furthermore, Worthy and Wise Perfected One, it is like a man at a crossroads who carries a vessel of fine food to sell. A traveler, weary and hungry from a long journey, sees the fine food and asks, "What is this?"

The food merchant replies, "This food is fragrant and delicious. If you eat it, it will relieve hunger and thirst, and you will behold the immortals. It has but one flaw: it brings the end of life."

Hearing this, the man thought, "I have no use for immortals, nor do I wish to die." He then said, "If eating this food will end my life, why do you sell it?"

The food merchant replied, "Those with wisdom will not buy it. Only the foolish, unaware of this matter, pay me much and greedily consume the food."

Perfected One and Daoist, it is likewise with you: you do not wish to be born in heaven, but only to escape affliction. Why is this?

For they cannot escape the manifold sufferings. Ordinary beings, wherever they are born, greedily cling to all sensations, for they do not see old age, sickness, and death.

Furthermore, good son, just as the sap of a poisonous tree’s root can kill a man, so too does the true person and Daoist, in the place of rebirth, receive the five thieving aggregates of form—all of which can kill. Furthermore, good son, just as excrement stinks whether much or little, so too is birth for the true person and Daoist: whether one lives a thousand years or as few as ten, suffering is inescapable.

Furthermore, wise and true person, it is like a perilous place where grass overhangs the bank, beneath which lies abundant sweet dew. If one eats it, one will live a thousand years, forever free from illness, secure and joyful. Ordinary beings, craving its taste, do not know that a deep pit lies below. They advance to take it, unaware, and fall into the pit, losing their lives. The wise, knowing this, abandon and keep far away.

The true person and Daoist are likewise: they do not even wish to receive the wondrous foods of the heavens, let alone those of the human realm. How could ordinary beings, who in hell gulp down molten iron, refrain from eating the fine delicacies of humans and gods? Through such parables—and countless others beyond measure—the true person and Daoist come to know that birth is great suffering.

The true person and Daoist, called the sage, abide in the Primordial Yang World, reciting the profound and subtle scriptures of the Great Vehicle, contemplating the suffering of birth.

How does the sage in the Primordial Yang World contemplate the suffering of old age? It brings coughing, wheezing, and shortness of breath; it steals the vigor of memory, the strength of youth, and the freedom of joyful abandon. Then comes the suffering of old age: flesh and skin wither, the body grows foul and stinking, and one is despised by others.

True person and Daoist, it is like a lotus in a pond, radiant in color, fresh and fragrant, beloved—until suddenly hail falls, and all at once it is scattered and ruined. Old age is likewise: it utterly destroys the beauty and strength of youth.

Furthermore, wise and true person, it is like a kingdom with a loyal minister, skilled in warfare. When another kingdom resists and refuses to submit to the king’s authority, the king sends the loyal minister to subdue it. He captures and binds the rebels and brings them before the king. Old age is likewise: it seizes the strength and beauty of youth and delivers them to the king.

Furthermore, good son, it is like a broken wheel, no longer of any use. The approach of old age is the same.

Furthermore, good son, it is like a great wealthy household, abundant in treasures, clothing, and bedding. Bandits break in, plunder the family’s wealth, and leave it utterly empty. The prime of youth and beauty is likewise: it is constantly plundered by the thief of old age.

Furthermore, good son, it is like a poor man who covets fine clothing but, though he longs for it, cannot obtain it. Old age is likewise: though one may still crave wealth and pleasure, one cannot attain them.

Furthermore, good son, it is like a land tortoise longing for water. The true person and Daoist, when dried up by the thief of decay, likewise long for the wealth and pleasures of their youth.

Furthermore, true person and Daoist, it is like the lotuses of autumn, once beloved, which wither and fade, so that people no longer wish to see them. The beauty and strength of youth are likewise: once cherished, when old age arrives, they are despised and scorned.

Furthermore, true person and Daoist, it is like sugarcane: once pressed, it loses its sweetness. The beauty of youth is likewise: once crushed by the thief of old age, it loses the flavor of the Dao, the taste of diligent study and recitation in renunciation.

Furthermore, good son, it is like the full moon in its brightest splendor, which yet does not shine by day. Old age is likewise: in youth, one is upright, one’s appearance fresh and pure; in old age, one becomes gaunt, one’s countenance withered and emaciated.

Furthermore, good son, it is like a king who rules his kingdom with true Dharma, governing the people without injustice. When enemy kingdoms invade, he is driven into exile, fleeing far into foreign lands. The people of those lands, seeing him, pity him and say, “Great king, you once ruled with true Dharma and did not oppress your subjects. How is it that in a single day you have been driven into exile, reduced to this?” A person is likewise: once ravaged by old age, they constantly recall the deeds of their youth.

Furthermore, good son, it is like a lamp’s wick, which depends entirely on oil. When the oil is exhausted, the flame cannot last long. Old age is likewise: it depends on the brightness of oil, but when the oil is gone, how can the wick of decay remain bright?

Furthermore, true person and Daoist, it is like a dried-up pond, which can no longer nourish people, non-humans, birds, or beasts. Old age is likewise: dried up by decay, it can no longer benefit the activities of life.

Furthermore, true person and Daoist, it is like a tree on a riverbank at the edge of a cliff. If a strong wind blows, it will surely fall. Old age is likewise: at the perilous bank of old age, a gust of wind arises, and the momentum of a lifetime cannot hold it back.

Furthermore, true person and Daoist, it is like a cart without wheels, unfit for carrying loads. Old age is likewise: it cannot receive the natural Dharma.

Furthermore, true person and Daoist, it is like an infant, despised by others. Old age is likewise: it is constantly scorned and belittled by all things.

Through these causes and parables—and countless others beyond measure and conception—such is the suffering of old age, truly a great affliction.

Noble son, this is called the true person and Daoist cultivating the *Primordial Yang Supreme Great Vehicle Scripture*, contemplating the suffering of old age.

At that time, the wise and virtuous true person asked: How does the noble son cultivate the *Primordial Yang Supreme Great Vehicle Wondrous Scripture*, contemplating the suffering of illness?

It is said that all illnesses can destroy every joyful affair, just as hail ruins the sprouts and grain, damaging the harvest. Furthermore, true person and Daoist, it is like a person with a grudge, constantly sorrowful and perpetually fearful. Noble son, all sentient beings and ordinary people are likewise: they are ever anxious about the suffering of illness, their hearts filled with sorrow.

Furthermore, true person and Daoist, it is like a person of upright appearance, favored by the king’s consort, desired and summoned by her messenger to consort with her. When the king learns of this, he gouges out one of the person’s eyes, cuts off one ear, severs one hand, and hacks off one foot. At that time, the person’s form and bearing are altered, and they are scorned by others. Noble son, illness is likewise: though one was once upright with all six senses intact, once afflicted by the suffering of illness, one is despised in the hearts of others.

Furthermore, true person and Daoist, it is like the plantain and the mule that bear offspring, only to be pressed by death. Noble son, illness is likewise: where there is illness, there is death.

Furthermore, true person and Daoist, it is like the Wheel-Turning King, whose generals and ministers always lead the way, with the king following behind; or like the four kinds of animal kings, with merchants leading the way, and all others following without exception. Noble son, the death Wheel-Turning King is likewise: he always has ministers of illness who do not abandon him, and the four kinds of merchants are likewise, perpetually pursued by the hosts of death.

True person and Daoist, the causes and conditions of illness are such: sorrow, fear, anxiety, and dread for body and mind; or being harassed by the enmity of thieves, shattering the floating gourd, piercing the ladder and steps, able to deceive and seize the root of right mindfulness, and to destroy beauty and angry countenance, scorching the body and damaging the skin. From these and other parables, know that the suffering of illness is a great thief.

Noble son, this is called the true person and Daoist who can practice the *Primordial Yang Great Vehicle Subtle and Wondrous Scripture*, contemplating the suffering of illness.

At that time, the wise and virtuous true person asked: How does the noble son cultivate and recite the *Primordial Yang Supreme Great Vehicle Scripture*, contemplating the suffering of death?

It is said that the suffering of death can destroy all things. True person and Daoist, it is like the arising of the kalpa fire, which can burn everything. Noble son, the fire of death is likewise: it can burn everything, except for the true person and Daoist abiding in the Primordial Yang world, for the power of the Heavenly Worthy does not reach them.

Furthermore, true person and Daoist, it is like the arising of a flood, which sweeps everything away, except for the three immortals, for its power does not reach them. Noble son, the water of death is likewise: it sweeps everything away, except for the true person abiding in the Primordial Yang realm of the Great Vehicle world.

Furthermore, true person and Daoist, it is like the arising of a wind disaster, which can shake and destroy all things, except for the four immortals, for its power does not reach them. Noble son, when the wind of death arrives, it can blow away all that exists, except for the true person and Daoist abiding in the Primordial Yang realm of the Great Vehicle world.

The wise and virtuous true person addressed the Heavenly Worthy, saying: By what cause and condition are the four immortals not consumed by fire, not drowned by water, and not shaken by wind?

The Heavenly Worthy replied to the wise and virtuous true person: The power of the four immortals lies in their being free from all faults. Noble son, the first immortal’s fault lies in internal observation, while externally there is the fire disaster; the second immortal’s fault lies in internal joy, while externally there is the water disaster; the third immortal’s fault lies in internal panting, while externally there is the wind disaster. Noble son, those four immortals have no leaks in their internal and external faults, and thus the disasters cannot reach them.

Noble son, the true person and Daoist are likewise: abiding in the Primordial Yang realm of the Great Vehicle world, they have completely eliminated all internal and external faults, and thus death cannot reach them.

Furthermore, true person and Daoist, it is like the golden-winged bird that can devour and digest all birds, fish, gold, silver, and other things, except for the vajra, which it cannot swallow. True person and Daoist, the golden-winged bird of death is likewise: it can devour all sentient beings, except it cannot consume the true person and Daoist abiding in the Primordial Yang realm of the Great Vehicle world.

Furthermore, wise and virtuous true person, it is like the trees on a perilous bank, all swept away by the rising floodwaters and carried into the great sea, except for the willow tree, because it is supple. Noble son, all sentient beings are likewise: they all flow into the sea of death, except for the true person and Daoist abiding in the Primordial Yang realm of the Great Vehicle world.

Furthermore, wise and virtuous true person, it is like the celestial warrior who can subdue all demons and ghosts, except for the great wind. Why? Because it is unimpeded. Noble son, the celestial warrior of death is likewise: it can subdue all ordinary sentient beings, except for the true person and Daoist abiding in the Primordial Yang realm of the Great Vehicle world. For what reason? Because they are unimpeded.

Furthermore, wise and virtuous true person, there are those who, amidst enmity and hatred, falsely feign kindness and constantly follow their foe, like an echo following sound, awaiting an opportunity to kill them. Yet the enemy remains vigilant, steadfast in self-defense, thus preventing harm. Noble son, the enmity of death is likewise: it lies in wait for all ordinary sentient beings, seeking to kill them, yet it cannot slay the true person and Daoist abiding in the Primordial Yang realm of the Great Vehicle world. Why? Because such true persons do not wander in heedlessness.

Furthermore, wise and virtuous true person, it is like a sudden torrential rain that destroys mountains, forests, trees, earth, stones, and gravel—all things—yet cannot harm the vajra jewel. Noble son, the vajra of death is likewise: it can annihilate all ordinary sentient beings, except for the vajra-like true person and Daoist abiding in the Primordial Yang realm of the Great Vehicle world.

Furthermore, wise and virtuous true person, it is like the golden-winged bird that can spy dragons and fish yet cannot devour the masters of the Three Caverns. Noble son, the golden-winged bird of death is likewise: it can consume countless sentient beings, all living creatures, except for the true person and Daoist who abide in the Three Caverns. What are the Three Caverns? They are the marks of emptiness and nothingness.

Furthermore, wise and virtuous true person, it is like a venomous serpent that releases its poison to sting a person. Though there may be potent incantations and worldly herbs, none can counter it—only the sacred ambrosial medicine of a sage can neutralize its venom. Noble son, the harm of death’s poison is likewise: all remedies are powerless against it, except for the true person abiding in the Primordial Yang realm of the Great Vehicle world.

Furthermore, wise and virtuous true person, it is like a man who has incurred the wrath of an official. If he humbles himself with soft words, offers apologies, and repays with treasures, he may yet escape. Noble son, the coercion of the official of death is not so: though one may offer soft words and treasures, death cannot be evaded. Noble son, death is this: in perilous and desolate places, without sustenance, far from others and without companions, the heart is filled with affliction, not knowing where to turn. It is deep, dark, and lightless, entering without a door yet having a place, though wracked with pain, it cannot be healed. There is no barrier to halt it; once gone, there is no return, and nothing is destroyed. Those who witness it grieve, though it is not an evil sight, it inspires terror. It spreads upon the body, yet cannot be perceived. Wise and virtuous true person, know that these similes describe death as the greatest suffering.

Moreover, wise one, this is how the true person and Daoist cultivate the supreme scriptures of the Primordial Yang Great Vehicle, contemplating the suffering of death. Then the wise and virtuous true person asked: How does the true person and Daoist, in cultivating the supreme scriptures of the Primordial Yang Great Vehicle, regard the suffering of parting from love? For it is the root of all suffering for ordinary beings. From this arises sorrow, and from sorrow, sentient beings grow old. The suffering of parting from love is the end of life. Noble son, through separation, all manner of suffering arises, and all manner of subtle afflictions. Now I shall explain each in detail.

Noble son, in a past age, human lifespans were long. After immeasurable kalpas, there was a king named Receptive Vision. When he was still a crown prince and later ascended the throne, his lifespan was forty thousand years. Upon the king’s head grew a fleshy topknot, soft as fine cotton, gradually growing without causing distress. After a full year, the topknot opened, and a boy was born, his form and countenance unmatched in the world, his beauty and bearing peerless among men. The king rejoiced and named him Adorned. King Receptive Vision then entrusted the affairs of state to him.

Adorned abandoned the kingdom’s treasures, his wives, children, and retinue, and entered the wondrous Joyous Mountain in the Pulin world to study the supreme scriptures of the Primordial Yang Great Vehicle. After a thousand years, Adorned, in the space of a month, stood upon a high terrace, bathed, donned his crown and robes, and received the precepts. Then, from the eastern direction, in the Land of Great Blessing of the Nasana world, a golden wheel-treasure chariot appeared, its hub and axle complete, naturally resplendent without need of craftsmanship, coming to him in response. King Adorned thought: *In a past kalpa, I heard a sage of the six penetrations say that if a universal monarch, in the space of a month, stands upon a high terrace, bathes, and receives the precepts, and if a golden wheel-treasure chariot, its hub and axle complete, naturally resplendent without craftsmanship, comes in response, then that king shall become a sage emperor of humans and gods.*

He then thought: *I shall test this.* Taking the wheel-treasure in his left hand and a censer in his right, he knelt, bowed in reverence, and made this vow: *If this wheel-treasure is true and not false, may it follow the path of the sage emperors of humans and gods of the past.* Having made this vow, the wheel-treasure soared into the sky, filling the ten directions, then returned to rest in Adorned’s left hand. Overjoyed, Adorned exclaimed: *Now I shall surely become a sage emperor of humans and gods.*

Soon after, a golden treasure appeared, its form majestic, like the petals of a lotus, its seven branches resting upon the earth. Seeing this, Adorned thought: *In a past kalpa, I heard a sage of the six penetrations say that if a sage emperor of humans and gods, in the space of a month, stands upon a high terrace, bathes, and receives the precepts, and if a golden elephant, its form majestic like the petals of a lotus, its seven branches resting upon the earth, comes in response, then that king is indeed a sage emperor of humans and gods.*

Again he thought: *Now I shall put this to the test.* Holding an incense burner in his left hand, he knelt on his right knee and made this vow: *If this golden treasure-elephant is true and not false, let it follow the Way as the sage emperors of humans and gods did in the past.*

Having made this vow, the golden treasure-elephant, from dawn until dusk, traversed the ten directions, reaching even the farthest shores of the great ocean, before returning to its original place. Then King Adorned, his heart filled with joy, leapt with boundless delight and declared: *Now I am surely a sage emperor of humans and gods.*

Soon after, a silver treasure-horse appeared, its form majestic, its mane and tail golden. Seeing this, Adorned thought: *In the past, I heard a sage of the six penetrations say that if a sage emperor of humans and gods, within a month, stands upon a high terrace, bathes, and receives the precepts, and if a silver treasure-horse, its color resplendent, its mane and tail golden, comes in response, then that king is indeed a sage emperor.*

Again he thought: *Now I shall put this to the test.* Holding an incense burner, he knelt in reverence and made this vow: *If this treasure-horse is true and not false, let it follow the Way as the sage emperors of humans and gods did in the past.*

Having made this vow, the treasure-horse galloped from dawn until dusk, traversing the ten directions, reaching even the farthest shores of the great ocean, before returning to its original place. Then Adorned, his heart leaping with joy, was filled with boundless delight and declared: *Now I am surely a sage emperor of humans and gods.*

Soon after, a woman of exquisite beauty appeared, her bearing radiant as jade, her countenance unmatched. From every pore of her body emanated rare fragrances; her breath was pure and sweet. Her eyes could see for ten *yojanas*, her ears, nose, and mind were keen and wise, and she possessed great wisdom. To all beings, she spoke gentle words. When her hand touched the king’s robe, she knew whether his future would be peaceful or troubled, and she discerned the thoughts of his heart.

Then Adorned thought: *If a woman can know the king’s heart, she is indeed the treasure of women.*

Soon after, within the king’s palace, a precious jewel appeared of its own accord—a great luminous pearl that could illuminate an entire world in darkness. If demon-kings or evil spirits sought to disturb the world’s ordinary beings, this pearl’s light would reveal them, and the demons could not draw near.

Then Adorned thought: *If a sage emperor of humans and gods obtains this treasure-pearl, he is surely a sage emperor.*

Soon after, a wise minister of the treasury appeared spontaneously, increasing the store of treasures until the royal vaults were filled to abundance, with nothing lacking. His eyes could perceive all hidden treasures throughout the ten directions of the world, and whatever the king desired would appear at the thought of it.

Then Adorned wished to test him. Boarding a ship, he sailed upon the great ocean and said to the treasury minister: *Now I shall follow you into the sea. Whatever I seek, I wish to obtain precious treasures.* Hearing the king’s words, the minister stirred the seawater with his hand, and from his fingertips emerged treasures, which he offered to the sage king, saying: *Great king, whatever you desire, I shall provide according to your will.*

Then Adorned, his heart filled with great joy, thought: *Now I am surely a sage emperor of humans and gods.*

Soon after, a minister of military wisdom appeared, a natural counselor, foremost in courage and strength, unmatched in wisdom. Skilled in strategy and well-versed in military arts, he could subdue any unruly kingdom and defend those already subdued.

Then Adorned thought: *If a sage emperor of humans and gods has such a minister to guard him, he is indeed a sage emperor.*

Then Adorned, the sage emperor of humans and gods, addressed his ministers and counselors: *Know this: in this Jambudvīpa world, peace and blessed happiness are now mine. My treasures are complete, and I lack nothing.*

The ministers replied: *The great world of the Eastern Hall of Fortune has not yet submitted to your virtue. Let the great king go personally to teach and transform that land.*

Then the sage king Adorned, taking with him the seven treasures, rare objects, and his retinue, went to that land. The people rejoiced and all submitted to his rule. Again he addressed his ministers: *Now my kingdom is at peace, my people prosperous, the seven treasures abundant, and my ministers and subjects complete. What more is there to do?*

The ministers replied: *The flourishing world of the West has not yet submitted.*

Then the sage king Adorned, again taking the seven treasures, rare objects, and his retinue, went to that land. The people rejoiced and all submitted. Again he addressed his ministers: *Now my kingdom is at peace, my people prosperous, the seven treasures abundant, and my ministers and subjects complete. What more is there to do?*

The ministers replied: *The pure world of the North has not yet submitted to your rule.*

Then the sage king Adorned, again taking the seven treasures, rare objects, and his retinue, went to that land. The people rejoiced and all submitted. Again he addressed his ministers: *Now my kingdom is at peace, my people prosperous, the seven treasures abundant, and my ministers and subjects complete. What more is there to do?*

The ministers replied: *The world of the Southern Abandoned Worthies has not yet submitted to your transformation.*

Then the sage king Adorned, again taking the seven treasures, rare objects, and his retinue, went to that land. The people rejoiced and all submitted. Again he addressed his ministers: *Now my kingdom is at peace, my people prosperous, the seven treasures abundant, and my ministers and subjects complete. What more is there to do?*

The ministers replied: *The Thirty-Two Heavens enjoy long life, peace, and bliss. Their celestial forms are majestic and unmatched, their dwellings adorned with precious treasures. Relying on their blessed happiness, they have not yet come to submit. Now you should go and make them submit.*

Then the sage king Adorned, again taking the seven treasures, rare objects, and his retinue, soared through the empty sky and ascended to the world of the Thirty-Two Heavens. There he saw a tree with lush branches and leaves, its fruits abundant. The sage king, upon seeing it, asked the Heavenly Lord, Divine Treasure Heavenly King: *What kind of tree is this?*

The Divine Treasure Heavenly King replied: *This is called the Qian Tree. The gods and immortals of the heavens gather beneath it in all seasons and months, delighting in joy and pleasure.*

Again, he saw clouds of a hue like purple qi and asked the Divine Treasure Heavenly King: *What is that color?*

He replied: *That is the Hall of Good Fortune. The celestial immortals and sages constantly gather there to discuss the affairs of humans and heavens.*

Thereupon, the Divine Treasure Heavenly King, recognizing the great power and virtue of King Adorned, ascended with him to the Hall of Good Fortune, where they sat upon separate seats. At that time, the two kings’ forms and appearances were equal, without difference.

Then the sage king thought to himself: *If I were to yield my throne to him and remain here, would I become the Heavenly King?*

O perfected ones and Daoists, at that time the Divine Treasure Heavenly King, through reciting and upholding the supreme, subtle scriptures of Primordial Yang, mastered their profound meanings and expounded their causes and conditions for others with great eloquence. By virtue of this recitation and teaching, he possessed great power and virtue.

Good son, King Adorned, harboring ill will toward the Divine Treasure Heavenly King, immediately fell from the heavens and returned to the world of Jambudvīpa. There, separated from what he loved and cherished, he was overcome by great suffering and affliction. Stricken by grave illness, he soon died.

At that time, the Most High Lord of the Dao declared: *I am that sage king of humans and heavens.*

Good son, know that the suffering of separation from what one loves is an extreme affliction.

Good son, if even perfected ones recall such past events—the suffering of separation from love—how much more should perfected ones dwelling in the land of Primordial Yang, the Mahāyāna world, observe the suffering of separation in the present age?

Good son, how do perfected ones and Daoists, cultivating the supreme Mahāyāna scriptures of Primordial Yang, contemplate the suffering of meeting what one hates?

Good son, these perfected ones and Daoists observe that in the heavens, the human realm, hells, the animal realm, and the realm of hungry ghosts, all beings experience the suffering of meeting what they hate—just as one might observe prisoners bound in shackles and fetters, weighed down by sorrow and suffering. So too do perfected ones and Daoists observe that all beings in the six destinies are bound in the meeting of hatred, enduring great suffering.

Furthermore, good son, it is like one who constantly fears an enemy and abandons parents, wife, children, property, and possessions to flee far away.

Good son, so too do perfected ones and Daoists, fearing the cycle of birth and death, cultivate the six perfections in full, seeking the supreme fruit of the Dao.

Good son, this is how perfected ones and Daoists, cultivating the supreme Mahāyāna scriptures of Primordial Yang, contemplate the suffering of meeting what one hates.

Good son, how do perfected ones cultivate the supreme Mahāyāna scriptures of Primordial Yang to contemplate the suffering of not obtaining what one seeks?

All beings seek without obtaining. This "seeking without obtaining" has two meanings: first, seeking wholesome dharmas without success; second, seeking unwholesome dharmas without fulfillment. Failing to escape the suffering of evil dharmas—this is a brief explanation of the intense suffering of the five aggregates.

Good son, this is what perfected ones and Daoists call the suffering of not obtaining what one seeks.

Then the wise and virtuous perfected one addressed the Heavenly Worthy: *As the Heavenly Worthy has said, the intense suffering of the five aggregates is not so. Why? As the Heavenly Worthy once told Fuzi Yuan: If form were suffering, ordinary beings would not seek it; if they did seek it, how could it be called suffering?*

As the Heavenly Worthy taught the Daoists: *There is suffering in sensation, joy in sensation, and neither suffering nor joy in sensation.*

As the Heavenly Worthy previously explained to the Daoist women, good sons, and good daughters: *One who cultivates wholesome dharmas attains joy.*

And as the Heavenly Worthy said: *In the path of goodness, the six defilements bring joy. The eye delights in forms—this is joy; the ear delights in sounds—this is joy; the nose delights in fragrances—this is joy; the tongue delights in flavors—this is joy; the body delights in smoothness—this is joy; the mind delights in glory—this is joy. The six senses’ delight in pleasant dharmas is all suffering.*

Then the Heavenly Worthy spoke this verse:

*Upholding precepts brings joy,
the body free from manifold suffering.
Reciting the Primordial Yang scriptures,
one enters the gate of immortality.
Hearing the scriptures, the suffering of meeting hatred
is cured as illness vanishes.
Cherishing the Dharma, love is like a floating gourd—
demons and ghosts cannot corrupt it.
Expounding the teachings, one becomes a sage king,
a compassionate father through causes and conditions.
Beware of harboring evil thoughts,
for evil topples even heavenly lords.
If one pities all beings,
using the Dharma as pillar and beam,
day and night ever reverent,
one may escape the Three Bureaus’ judgment.
Perfected ones and Daoists,
strike the Dharma drum with devotion.
Awakening to the six destinies,
ever keep the Dharma as your companion.
The Mahāyāna is the world,
heavenly realms its pure land.
All roots of sin are exhausted,
all by my rule and measure.*

When the Heavenly Worthy had finished speaking this verse, the meaning of these matters was as expounded in the scriptures. The wise and virtuous perfected ones, having heard this teaching, understood it clearly, bowed in reverence, and withdrew.

Thus concludes the first scroll of *The Supreme Numinous Treasure Scripture of Primordial Yang*.

Then the Most High Jade Clarity Heavenly Worthy addressed the Primordial Yang Settled Light Perfected One, saying: I observe all dharmas to be impermanent, their natures and characteristics unfixed. Any dharma arising from causes is thereby impermanent. All these immortals possess nothing that does not arise from conditions.

Good man, the nature of the Dao is neither hidden nor manifest, neither generated nor extinguished, neither departing nor arriving. It is not present, not future, not past; it is not produced by causes nor born of conditions. Therefore it is called eternal.

Good man, the nature of the Dao is the Heavenly Worthy; the Heavenly Worthy is the Dharma; the Dharma is the eternal. Good man, the eternal is the Heavenly Worthy; the Heavenly Worthy is the Perfected One; the Perfected One is the eternal; the nature of the Dao is the eternal. For this reason, dharmas arising from causes are not called eternal. Among the lesser immortals, nothing exists that does not arise from causes.

Good man, these lesser immortals do not perceive the nature of the Dao, the wondrous Dharma of the Heavenly Worthy. Therefore, all that lesser immortals discuss is delusion, devoid of truth. All beings first see clothing, bedding, carriages, elephants, horses, cities, and villages; later, seeing similar things, they claim these are eternal. Know that in truth they are not eternal!

Good man, all conditioned dharmas are impermanent. The unconditioned void is therefore eternal. The nature of the Dao is unconditioned, therefore eternal. The void is the nature of the Dao; the nature of the Dao is the Heavenly Worthy; the Heavenly Worthy is the unconditioned; the unconditioned is eternal bliss; eternal bliss is the Dharma; the Dharma is the unconditioned; the unconditioned is eternal bliss.

Good man, conditioned dharmas are of two kinds: first, form-dharmas; second, non-form-dharmas. Non-form-dharmas are mind and mental factors; form-dharmas are earth, water, fire, and wind.

Good man, mind is called impermanent. Why? Its nature is that of grasping and conditioning, requiring differentiation. Good man, the nature of eye-consciousness differs, even to the nature of mind-consciousness differing—therefore it is impermanent. Good man, the realm of form differs, even to the realm of dharmas differing—therefore it is impermanent. Good man, the correspondence of the eye-faculty differs, even to the correspondence of the mind-faculty and consciousness differing—therefore it is impermanent.

Good man, if mind were eternal, the eye-faculty would cognize all dharmas alone. Good man, if the eye-faculty’s cognition differs from the mind-faculty’s cognition, know that it is impermanent. Through the similarity of dharmas, arising and ceasing moment to moment, the common person, seeing this, calculates it as eternal.

Good man, all conditioned phenomena are destructible, hence also called impermanent. That is to say, eye-consciousness arises from the causes of eye, form, light, clarity, and contemplation. When ear-consciousness arises, its causes differ, not those of eye-consciousness. Even to mind-consciousness, it is likewise.

Furthermore, Settled Light Perfected One, good men who harbor conditioned actions—mind is therefore called impermanent. That is to say, the mind cultivating impermanence differs; the mind cultivating suffering, emptiness, and non-self differs. If mind were eternal, it would constantly cultivate impermanence, yet still not perceive suffering, emptiness, and non-self—how much less would it perceive the bliss of true eternity! For this reason, the teachings of lesser immortals cannot encompass the bliss of true eternity.

Good man, know that the mind-dharma is certainly impermanent. Furthermore, good man, the nature of mind differs, hence it is called impermanent. That is to say, those of perverse views and doubts have minds of differing natures: they do not believe in the true Dharma, do not revere true Perfected Ones, do not honor those with the Dao, and harbor evil thoughts.

Good man, know that the function of mind has three kinds: first, the mind of one who has left the household; second, the mind of one who remains in the household; third, the mind of one who has departed from the household in violation. The mind corresponding to joy differs; the mind corresponding to suffering differs; the mind corresponding to neither suffering nor joy differs; the mind corresponding to greed differs; the mind corresponding to anger differs; the mind corresponding to delusion differs; the mind corresponding to all perverse views and demonic influences also differs.

That is to say, the mind corresponding to delusion differs; the mind corresponding to doubt differs; the mind corresponding to perverse views differs. In movement and stillness, action and conduct, the mind also differs. Good man, if mind were truly eternal, it could not differentiate various forms—such as dark and light, vermilion and purple, red and white.

Good man, if mind were eternal, it would not forget the dharmas it recalls. Good man, if mind were eternal, what one studies and recites would not increase. Furthermore, good man, if mind were eternal, one would not speak of what has been done and what is being done. If there is what has been done and what is being done, know that this mind is certainly impermanent.

Good man, if mind were eternal, there would be no enmity or kinship, neither enmity nor kinship. If mind were true, one would not speak of "my things" and "others' things," of arising and ceasing. If mind were true, though it had done something, it would not endure. Good man, for this reason, know that the nature of mind differs in each case. Because of these differences, know that there is no truth.

Good man, I have now expounded the absence of truth in non-form-dharmas, and its meaning is manifest. I shall further explain to you the absence of truth in form. Form is without truth, originally without arising; once arisen, it ceases—hence know it is without truth. The ignorant common person calculates it as true. For this reason, it is called without truth. If without truth, it is without suffering; if without suffering, it is not impure.

Good man, in the primordial movement of the Dao, I first inquired into this matter and thereby attained clear understanding.

Furthermore, good man, all dharmas are without self. In all matters—whether form or non-form—form is not the self. Why? Because it can be destroyed, extinguished, broken, or damaged, and because it arises and grows. The self, however, cannot be destroyed, extinguished, broken, or damaged, nor can it grow. For this reason, know that form is not the self. Non-form dharmas are also not the self. Why? Because they arise from conditions.

Good man, if those of perverse views, through single-minded concentration, know there is a self, the nature of single-minded concentration is not truly the self. If single-minded concentration were the self, its nature—being a past phenomenon—would entail forgetfulness. Since there is forgetfulness, know decisively that there is no self.

Good man, if those of perverse views, through recollection, know there is a self, then without recollection, know decisively that there is no self. As when one sees a person with six fingers and asks, "Where did I first meet you?" If there were a self, one would not need to ask. Because one asks, know decisively that there is no self.

Good man, if those of perverse views, through negation, know there is a self—good man, because there is negation, know decisively that there is no self. As when one says "child," it is the same with me. If it were truly the self, it would never be negated. Because it is negated, know decisively that there is no self. If negation were the means to know there is a self, then when you do not negate, there should decisively be no self.

Good man, if those of perverse views, through companionship or lack thereof, know there is a self, then because there is no companion, there should be no self. The dharma without companionship is called the Heavenly Venerable; the empty and void nature of the Dao is also thus—truly without companionship. For this reason, know decisively that there is no self.

Furthermore, good man, if those of perverse views, through the fixation of names, know there is a self, then in the dharma without self, there is also the name of self—like a poor and lowly person named "Noble and Wealthy." As when one says, "I die," if there were a self, it would kill me, yet the self cannot truly be killed; it is only nominally said to be killed. It is also like a seated person called a "great elder." For this reason, know decisively that there is no self.

Furthermore, good man, if those of perverse views, through a newborn seeking milk, know there is a self—good man, if there were a self, no infant would grasp impurity. For this reason, know decisively that there is no self.

Furthermore, good man, all beings possess equal wisdom in three matters: sexual misconduct, eating, and fear. Therefore, there is no self.

If those of perverse views, through appearance, know there is a self, then because of appearance there is no self, and because of no appearance there is also no self. When a person sleeps, they cannot move forward or stop, look up or down, see or blink, nor know suffering or joy—there should be no self. If through moving forward or stopping, looking up or down, seeing or blinking, one knows there is a self, then wooden puppets should also have a self.

Good man, neither moving nor stopping, neither looking up nor down, neither seeing nor blinking, neither suffering nor joy, neither greed nor anger, neither delusion nor action—such is the true Dao. The true self exists thus. If beings of perverse views, upon seeing others eat fruit, inevitably feel envy in their mouths, and thereby know there is a self—good man, because of recollection, seeing gives rise to envy. Envy is not the self, nor is the self envy; it is neither joy nor pleasure. For this reason, know decisively that there is no self.

Good man, such beings are as ignorant as infants. They lack wisdom and skillful means, unable to comprehend the constant and the inconstant, suffering and non-suffering, joy and non-joy, purity and impurity, self and no-self, lifespan and non-lifespan, beings and non-beings. In the dharma of the Dao, they grasp a small, illusory portion and calculate it as true and constant joy, yet truly do not know true and constant joy.

Like a person born blind who does not recognize the color of milk and asks another, "What is the color of milk like?" The other replies, "The color of milk is as white as a shell." The blind person asks again, "Is the color of milk like the sound of a shell?" The reply is, "No." The blind person asks further, "What is a shell like?" The reply is, "Like the taste of rice." The blind person asks again, "Is the color of milk as soft as the taste of rice? And what is the taste of rice like?" The reply is, "Like snow." The blind person asks again, "Is the taste of rice as cold as snow? And what is snow like?" The reply is, "Like a white crane." Though the blind person hears such analogies, they ultimately cannot recognize the true color of milk.

Such are beings of perverse views—they ultimately cannot recognize true and constant joy. For this reason, in my dharma of the Dao, there is the meaning of true constancy, not in perverse views.

Then the Perfected One, Radiant Light, addressed the Heavenly Venerable, saying: "Rare is the Great Master, who in the present and past turns the unsurpassed Dharma-wheel, making such distinctions."

The True and Constant Heavenly Venerable said to the Perfected One, Radiant Light: "Why do you now conceive of the Great Master as past? Good man, the Great Master is truly constant, unchanging and not passing away. If one calculates a self, this dharma becomes the supreme, correct, and true Great Dao.

I am the dharma, the dharma is my body;
I am the Dao, the Dao is my body;
I am the Great Master, the Great Master is my body;
I am the true immortal, the true immortal is my body.

I can expound the dharma, causing others to listen and receive; I turn the Dharma-wheel, which others cannot. The Great Master never makes such calculations, therefore the Great Master does not turn the Dharma-wheel."

If one entertains such deluded calculations as "I am the eye, the eye is me," the same applies to body, mouth, intent, ear, and nose. "I am form, form is my body," and so on up to the true Dharma, all follow this pattern. "I am earth, earth is my body," and likewise for water, fire, and wind.

If one claims, "I am speech, speech is my body; I am erudition, erudition is my body; I am generosity, generosity is my body; I am discipline, discipline is my body; I am forbearance, forbearance is my body; I am stillness, stillness is my body; I am diligence, diligence is my body; I am wisdom, wisdom is my body; I am the six recollections, the six recollections are my body"—the four right exertions, the four bases of miraculous power, the five pure mental states, and the eightfold noble path all follow this pattern.

Good man, the Great Master never makes such calculations, therefore the Great Master does not turn the Dharma-wheel. If one speaks of constancy, without change or alteration, how can it be said that the Great Master turns the Dharma-wheel? Thus, you should not now declare that the Great Master is turning the supreme Dharma-wheel.

Good man, it is like the eye depending on form, light, and contemplation; when conditions converge, visual consciousness arises. The eye does not think, "I generate consciousness," nor does contemplation think, "I produce visual consciousness," nor does it think, "I generate myself." Such phenomena, when conditions converge, are called "seeing."

Good man, the Heavenly Venerable is likewise: through the six perfections and the sevenfold Dharma, comprehending all phenomena, and further through the throat, lips, teeth, tongue, speech, and sound, the teaching begins—this is called "turning the Dharma-wheel." For this reason, the Heavenly Venerable is not said to turn the Dharma-wheel.

Good man, if there is no turning, this is called the Dharma; the Dharma is the Great Master.

Good man, it is like fire arising from the fire-drill, the hand, and dry grass: the drill does not say, "I generate fire," nor do the hand or grass say, "I generate fire," nor does the fire say, "I generate myself." The Heavenly Venerable is likewise: through the six perfections, up to being called "turning the Dharma-wheel," the Heavenly Venerable does not think, "I turn the Dharma-wheel."

Good man, if there is no such thought, this is called "turning the true Dharma-wheel"; this turning of the Dharma-wheel is the Heavenly Venerable, the Great Master.
